<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

				<strong>Abstract:</strong>
				<div class="abstract-text ng-binding" ng-bind-html="::vm.details.abstract">In this letter, a novel ultrahigh-frequency radio frequency identification (UHF RFID) tag antenna is proposed. Suitable for metallic objects, this broadband antenna can cover the three major UHF RFID bands. A modified folded dipole and a meander bar have been implemented on the upper substrate, while the second substrate is a double face with parallel bars printed on the top face and a metal plane on the bottom to isolate the antenna from metallic surfaces. The bars are used as parasitic elements to excite multiresonant modes and enhance the antenna bandwidth. To improve the gain of the antenna, the two substrates have been speared by an air layer. Furthermore, the proposed design is cost-effective since it does not require metallic vias. Mounted on a metallic plate of 300 × 250 mm<sup>2</sup>, the proposed antenna covers the targeted UHF RFID bands with a return loss better than 10 dB and a maximum outdoor measured reading range of 14.6 m at 905 MHz.</div>
			</div><!-- end ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract -->

<div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>In THE last few years, the ultrahigh-frequency radio frequency identification (UHF RFID) technology has found wide
 deployment in supply chain automation and traffic management, thanks to its long reading range and low cost 
<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>. However, such performance can be significantly worsened in the presence of
 metallic objects that degrade the radiation efficiency and gain of the tag antenna. Moreover, coupling between the
 conductive material and the tag can change its input impedance and reduce its bandwidth. Therefore, the challenge is
 to design UHF RFID tags that can still exhibit high performance while working near metallic surfaces.</p><p>The planar inverted-F antenna is one of the most proposed structures for UHF RFID tags on metallic objects. However,
 this structure generally suffers from a narrow bandwidth. In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a>, two adjacent
 resonant modes have been excited to enlarge the operating bandwidth by embedding slits into the upper horizontal strip
 of the inverted-E antenna.</p><p>However, covering both the European region band (860–870 MHz) and the American/Asian region bands
 (900–960 MHz), while maintaining a minimum input reflection coefficient of the antenna, remains a challenge 
<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a>.</p><p>In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a>, a metallic cavity has been employed with an artificial magnetic
 conductor ground plane installed on its bottom side and a bowtie antenna on the upper side. This approach can help
 improving the gain and the bandwidth, but it is still a costly and complex solution and not suitable for applications
 that require low-profile antennas. On the other side, a small antenna was designed in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">
[5]</a> with a dual-layer substrate and connected strips and vias to keep the same phase currents in the different
 strips. However, this type of antenna suffers from a narrow bandwidth and requires several metallic vias.</p><p>In this letter, a novel tag antenna design suitable for metallic objects is proposed. By embedding parasitic
 elements on the proposed antenna, multiresonant modes can be created to broaden the impedance bandwidth. Moreover, the
 gain could be improved by separating the radiating element from the ground plane with an air layer. These allow the
 designed tag to keep similar performances regardless of the nature of the attached objects (metallic or not). The
 proposed antenna covers the three major UHF RFID bands with a total volume of
 104 × 31 × 7.6 mm<sup>3</sup>. Maximum measured reading ranges of 11.8, 14.6, and
 6 m were achieved at 868, 905, and 945 MHz, respectively, with the antenna mounted on metallic plates of
 300 × 250 mm<sup>2</sup>.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Antenna Structure and Operation</h2></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2a"><h3>A. Antenna Structure and Design</h3><p>The structure of the proposed antenna is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a>. It is composed of
 two same-size layers of FR4 substrate, separated by air. The upper layer is a 0.8-mm single-face substrate with a
 modified dipole and a meander bar printed on the same face. Two slots have been inserted in each end of the dipole to
 reduce the antenna length. Also, two symmetrical slots have been etched in the center of the dipole to increase its
 inductance and match the impedance of the antenna to the chip impedance. The microstrip line between the two center
 slots, i.e., the feedline, has been extended in the two arms of the dipole to precisely adjust the impedance of the
 antenna. Therefore, the input impedance can be easily tuned by the input network formed by the feedline and the center
 slots. The second layer of the antenna is a 0.8-mm double-face FR4 substrate.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig1"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani1abc-2557778-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani1abc-2557778-small.gif" alt="Figure 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 1. </b><fig><p>Structure of the proposed antenna. (a) Top layer. (b) Second layer. (c) Side view.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7457626/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p><p>Parallel bars (parasitic elements) were printed on the top side of this second substrate to increase the bandwidth
 of the antenna by creating multiresonant modes in the targeted bands. Besides, a metallic plane has been inserted in
 the bottom side to keep the performance of the antenna even in the presence of metallic objects.</p><p>The antenna has been designed to conjugate the commercial microchip Alien Higgs-4 (from Alien Corporation's) 
<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_2a">[6]</a>, which has an input impedance <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX">
<span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1" role="math" style="width: 10.393em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; padding-left: 0.243em;">−</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-23" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic; padding-left: 0.243em;">j</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-24" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">180</span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-25" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span><span class="mspace" id="MathJax-Span-26" style="height: 0.003em; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.397em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.27em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-1">{Z_{{\rm{chip}}}} \approx ({20 - j180})\;{\rm{\Omega }}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and a reading sensitivity of
 approximately –18.5 dBm. Four holes have been etched at the ends of each substrate and four plastic screws
 employed to maintain a fixed distance between the two layers. Note that the proposed antenna with total volume of

width: 0px; height: 3.972em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.833em; left: 0.665em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-724"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-725"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-726"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-727"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-728"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-729"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-730" style="font-size: 70.7%; <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-36-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-746" role="math" style="width: 6.617em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 5.228em; height: 0px; font-size: 126%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.326em 1005.16em 2.649em -999.997em); top: -2.245em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-747"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-748"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-749"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-750"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.392em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.046em 1000.73em 4.17em -999.997em); top: -3.965em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-751" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">C<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.069em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.972em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.833em; left: 0.731em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-752"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-753"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-754"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-755"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-756" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">m</span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.972em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-757" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.268em;">=</span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-758" style="padding-left: 0.268em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-759"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-760"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-761"><span class="mspace" id="MathJax-Span-762" style="height: 0.003em; vertical-align: 0.003em; width: 0.268em; display: inline-block; overflow: hidden;"></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-763" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">15</span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-764"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-765"><span class="mspace" id="MathJax-Span-766" style="height: 0.003em; vertical-align: 0.003em; width: 0.268em; display: inline-block; overflow: hidden;"></span><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-767"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-768"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-769" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">pF</span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.252em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.329em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.338em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-36">{C_{\rm{m}}} = {\rm{\;}}15{\;\hbox{pF}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.</p>
</fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7457626/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p><p>Based on the above circuit, the input impedance of the tag was deduced and then successfully compared to the
 simulated one as shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3</a>, thus validating the proposed equivalent
 circuit. The proposed antenna exhibits four resonant frequencies located in the band of 817–970 MHz. The first
 resonance at 850 MHz is given by the bar on the upper layer. The second resonance at 880 MHz is due to the dipole. The
 third and the fourth resonances, occurring, respectively, at 925 and 945 MHz, are excited by the bars on the bottom
 layer. Therefore, by varying the lengths, each resonance could be easily adjusted to cover the three main UHF RFID
 region bands.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani3-2557778-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani3-2557778-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 3. </b><fig><p>Calculated and simulated antenna input impedance.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7457626/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Experimental Results</h2></div><p>To experimentally validate the proposed antenna design, measurements were performed with the fabricated prototype
 shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 4</a>. First, the input impedance of the fabricated antenna was
 measured using the port extension technique (differential input) <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_3">[8]</a>.
 Therefore, two VNA ports, a coaxial balun, and a port extension technique were used to precisely measure the input
 impedance of the proposed antenna.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig4"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani4-2557778-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani4-2557778-small.gif" alt="Figure 4" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 4. </b><fig><p>Photograph of the fabricated antenna.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7457626/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p><div class="section_2" id="sec3a"><h3>A. Input Impedance and Return Loss</h3><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 5</a> shows the measured and simulated input impedance of the prototype
 antenna (characterized in both open air and on a metallic plate with a surface of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-37-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-770" role="math" style="width: 7.39em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 6.67em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.204em 1006.67em 2.405em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-771"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-772" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">300</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-773" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">×</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-774" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">250</span><span class="mspace" id="MathJax-Span-775" style="height: 0.003em; vertical-align: 0.003em; width: 0.303em; display: inline-block; overflow: hidden;"></span><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-776"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 2.105em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.426em 1001.68em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-777"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-778"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-779" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">mm</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.381em; left: 1.685em;"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-780" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">2</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-37">300 \times 250\;\hbox{mm}^2</script></tex-math></inline-formula>). We can see that the input
 impedance results are very close regardless of the presence of metal. The antenna performs in almost similar ways with
 or without the presence of metallic objects. Also, the input impedance of the proposed antenna is about 
<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-38-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-781" role="math" style="width: 6.309em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 5.649em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1005.59em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-782"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-783" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">(</span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-784"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-785"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-786" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">20</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-787" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">+</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-788" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic; padding-left: 0.243em;">j</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-789" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">180</span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-790" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-791" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Ω</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.27em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-38">({20 + j180})\Omega </script></tex-math></inline-formula> among a large bandwidth,
 a value close to the conjugate impedance of the used chip. As shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig6" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 6</a>,
 measured and simulated return loss match quite well. Also, with a 10-dB return loss, the antenna behaves as a
 dual-band device by covering both the European and the American/Asian region bands, making it suitable for the three
 main operating UHF RFID region bands.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig5"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani5-2557778-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani5-2557778-small.gif" alt="Figure 5" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 5. </b><fig><p>Measured and simulated input impedance of the antenna in open air and on a metallic plate (<sup>*</sup>
 M: measured, S: simulated).</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7457626/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig6"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani6-2557778-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani6-2557778-small.gif" alt="Figure 6" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 6. </b><fig><p>Measured and simulated return loss of the proposed antenna in open air and on metallic plate.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7457626/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3b"><h3>B. Gain and Reading Range</h3><p>The antenna gain was measured under the conditions of minimum tag turn-on power. The reader antenna and the test tag
 were placed with a linear polarization in an anechoic chamber, at a fixed distance of 1 m. To further investigate the
 antenna performance, maximum reading range measurements for the prototype tag were performed in outdoor environment.
</p><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 7</a> displays the simulated and measured radiation patterns along <i>
xz</i>- and <i>yz</i>-planes at 868 MHz, on metal plate. The maximum radiation is in the +
<i>z</i>-direction. The measured and simulated gain values for the prototype antenna mounted on a metallic
 plate are shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 8</a> and summarized in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">
Table I</a> along with the measured maximum reading range.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig7"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani7-2557778-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani7-2557778-small.gif" alt="Figure 7" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 7. </b><fig><p>Simulated and measured radiation pattern along <i>xz</i>- and <i>yz</i>-planes at 868
 MHz, on metal plate.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7457626/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig8"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani8-2557778-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani8-2557778-small.gif" alt="Figure 8" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 8. </b><fig><p>Measured and simulated gain of the proposed antenna.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7457626/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full table" id="table1"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">TABLE I </b>Gain and Reading Range<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani.t1-2557778-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani.t1-2557778-small.gif" alt="Table I" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div><p></p><p>As reported, a maximum measured gain of 2.1 dB was achieved at 890 MHz. Also, prior simulations demonstrated that
 the antenna exhibits almost the same gain performance with different metal plate sizes. This is mainly because the
 antenna was designed and optimized with a ground plane in the lower substrate, while the metal plate under the antenna
 can be considered as an extension of this ground plane <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_3b">[14]</a>, 
<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_3b">[15]</a>.</p><p>Note that the reader emitted power was set to 3.3-W EIRP for the European band and 4-W EIRP for the American/Asian
 bands. At 868 MHz, the fabricated tag achieves a maximum readable range of 11.8 m. Note also that simulated and
 measured results do not match in the low-frequency band due to the losses of the reader antenna (the antenna used in
 measurements). The reader antenna works in wideband, from 700 MHz to 18 GHz, and presents some losses in the
 extremities of its operating band.</p><p>As shown in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table2" class="fulltext-link">Table II</a>, a successful performance comparison of the
 proposed antenna versus existing works was achieved. Also, even if some existing works (e.g., 
<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_3b">[5]</a>) claim smaller sizes, they used vias and specific substrate, which
 significantly increase the antenna cost. Furthermore, they do not cover the whole targeted UHF RFID region bands and
 exhibit a smaller reading range.
</p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table2"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">TABLE II </b>Performance Comparison to Published Works<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani.t2-2557778-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7855842/7457626/hamani.t2-2557778-small.gif" alt="Table II" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div><p></p><p>Our antenna covers the three major UHF RFID region bands while achieving good gain and reading ranges, and assures
 low-cost solution, making it a promising solution for broadband UHF RFID tags on metallic objects.</p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Conclusion</h2></div><p>In this letter, a novel broadband antenna for metallic objects is proposed. Embedded on two FR4 substrates and
 separated by an air layer, the antenna covers the major UHF RFID region bands with 10-dB return loss, both in open air
 and on metallic plate. It is of low profile and low cost and occupies a total volume of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-39-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-792" role="math" style="width: 9.553em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 8.592em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.204em 1008.59em 2.405em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-793"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-794" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">104</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-795" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">×</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-796" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">31</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-797" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">×</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-798" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">7.6</span><span class="mspace" id="MathJax-Span-799" style="height: 0.003em; vertical-align: 0.003em; width: 0.183em; display: inline-block; overflow: hidden;"></span><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-800"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 2.105em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.426em 1001.68em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-801"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-802"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-803"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-804"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-805" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">mm</span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.381em; left: 1.685em;"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-806" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">3</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-39">104 \times 31 \times 7.6\,{\hbox{mm}}^3</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. To broaden the
 impedance bandwidth, multiresonant modes were excited by embedding parasitic elements on the antenna. The proposed
 design approach was demonstrated through close agreement between simulated and measured data. In addition, good
 measured reading distances have been achieved when the prototype antenna is mounted on a metallic plate, making the
 proposed antenna quite suitable for broadband UHF RFID tags on metallic object applications.</p></div>

<script>

function getCookie(name) {
	var nameEQ = name + "=";
	var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
	for(var i=0;i &lt; ca.length;i++) {
		var c = ca[i];
		while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
		if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
	}
	return null;
}

		Modal.refreshLegacyAccountTransition('/xpl/mwLegacyAccountTransition.jsp');
		}
	}
});

</script>

novel ultrahigh-frequency radio frequency identification (UHF RFID) tag antenna is proposed. Suitable for metallic objects, this broadband antenna can cover the three major UHF RFID bands. A modified folded dipole and a meander bar have been implemented on the upper substrate, while the second substrate is a double face with parallel bars printed on the top face and a metal plane on the bottom to isolate the antenna from metallic surfaces. The bars are used as parasitic elements to excite multiresonant modes and enhance the antenna bandwidth. To improve the gain of the antenna, the two substrates have been speared by an air layer.
